<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Gamma-ray burst</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="hatnote" role="note">For bursts of gamma rays of terrestrial origin, see <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_gamma-ray_flash" title="Terrestrial gamma-ray flash">Terrestrial gamma-ray flash</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Fast_radio_burst" title="Fast radio burst">fast radio burst</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/X-ray_burst" title="X-ray burst">X-ray burst</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gamma_ray_burst.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1396" data-file-width="2000" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Gamma_ray_burst.jpg/300px-Gamma_ray_burst.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Gamma_ray_burst.jpg/450px-Gamma_ray_burst.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Gamma_ray_burst.jpg/600px-Gamma_ray_burst.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gamma_ray_burst.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's illustration showing the life of a <a href="/wiki/Star#Massive_stars" title="Star">massive star</a> as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> converts lighter elements into heavier ones. When fusion no longer generates enough pressure to counteract gravity, the star rapidly collapses to form a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>. Theoretically, energy may be released during the collapse along the axis of rotation to form a gamma-ray burst.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_astronomy" title="Gamma-ray astronomy">gamma-ray astronomy</a>, <b>Gamma-ray bursts</b> (<b>GRBs</b>) are extremely energetic explosions that have been observed in distant <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxies</a>. They are the brightest <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic</a> events known to occur in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Bursts can last from ten milliseconds to several hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> After an initial flash of <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, a longer-lived "afterglow" is usually emitted at longer wavelengths (<a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">optical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The intense radiation of most observed GRBs is believed to be released during a <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypernova" title="Hypernova">hypernova</a> as a rapidly rotating, high-mass <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> collapses to form a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron star</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quark_star" title="Quark star">quark star</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>. A subclass of GRBs (the "short" bursts) appear to originate from a different process: the merger of <a href="/wiki/Binary_star" title="Binary star">binary</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neutron_stars" title="Neutron stars">neutron stars</a>. The cause of the precursor burst observed in some of these short events may be the development of a resonance between the crust and core of such stars as a result of the massive tidal forces experienced in the seconds leading up to their collision, causing the entire crust of the star to shatter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The sources of most GRBs are billions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a> away from <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, implying that the explosions are both extremely energetic (a typical burst releases as much energy in a few seconds as the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> will in its entire 10-billion-year lifetime) and extremely rare (a few per galaxy per million years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rates_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-rates-7">[7]</a></sup>). All observed GRBs have originated from outside the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_galaxy" title="Milky Way galaxy">Milky Way galaxy</a>, although a related class of phenomena, <a href="/wiki/Soft_gamma_repeater" title="Soft gamma repeater">soft gamma repeater</a> flares, are associated with <a href="/wiki/Magnetar" title="Magnetar">magnetars</a> within the Milky Way. It has been hypothesized that a gamma-ray burst in the Milky Way, pointing directly towards the Earth, could cause a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_extinction" title="Mass extinction">mass extinction</a> event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melott2004_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Melott2004-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>GRBs were first detected in 1967 by the <a href="/wiki/Vela_(satellite)" title="Vela (satellite)">Vela satellites</a>, which had been designed to detect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">covert nuclear weapons tests</a>. Following their discovery, hundreds of theoretical models were proposed to explain these bursts, such as collisions between <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star" title="Neutron star">neutron stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Little information was available to verify these models until the 1997 detection of the first X-ray and optical afterglows and direct measurement of their <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshifts</a> using optical <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">spectroscopy</a>, and thus their distances and energy outputs. These discoveries, and subsequent studies of the galaxies and <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a> associated with the bursts, clarified the distance and luminosity of GRBs, definitively placing them in distant galaxies.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_gamma-ray_burst_research" title="History of gamma-ray burst research">History of gamma-ray burst research</a></div>
<p>Gamma-ray bursts were first observed in the late 1960s by the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Vela_(satellite)" title="Vela (satellite)">Vela</a> satellites, which were built to detect gamma radiation pulses emitted by nuclear weapons tested in space. The <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> suspected that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a> might attempt to conduct secret nuclear tests after signing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" title="Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a> in 1963. On July 2, 1967, at 14:19 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>, the Vela 4 and Vela 3 satellites detected a flash of gamma radiation unlike any known nuclear weapons signature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FlashVela_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FlashVela-10">[10]</a></sup> Uncertain what had happened but not considering the matter particularly urgent, the team at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_Scientific_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos Scientific Laboratory">Los Alamos Scientific Laboratory</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Ray_Klebesadel" title="Ray Klebesadel">Ray Klebesadel</a>, filed the data away for investigation. As additional Vela satellites were launched with better instruments, the Los Alamos team continued to find inexplicable gamma-ray bursts in their data. By analyzing the different arrival times of the bursts as detected by different satellites, the team was able to determine rough estimates for the <a href="/wiki/Star_position" title="Star position">sky positions</a> of sixteen bursts<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FlashVela_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FlashVela-10">[10]</a></sup> and definitively rule out a terrestrial or solar origin. The discovery was declassified and published in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BATSE_2704.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="691" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/BATSE_2704.jpg/250px-BATSE_2704.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/BATSE_2704.jpg/375px-BATSE_2704.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/BATSE_2704.jpg/500px-BATSE_2704.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BATSE_2704.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Positions on the sky of all gamma-ray bursts detected during the BATSE mission. The distribution is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isotropic" title="Isotropic">isotropic</a>, with no concentration towards the plane of the Milky Way, which runs horizontally through the center of the image.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most early theories of gamma-ray bursts posited nearby sources within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy" title="Milky Way Galaxy">Milky Way Galaxy</a>. From 1991, the <a href="/wiki/Compton_Gamma_Ray_Observatory" title="Compton Gamma Ray Observatory">Compton Gamma Ray Observatory</a> and its Burst and Transient Source Explorer (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGRO#Instruments" title="CGRO">BATSE</a>) instrument, an extremely sensitive gamma-ray detector, provided data that showed the distribution of GRBs is <a href="/wiki/Isotropy" title="Isotropy">isotropic</a>—not biased towards any particular direction in space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> If the sources were from within our own galaxy they would be strongly concentrated in or near the galactic plane. The absence of any such pattern in the case of GRBs provided strong evidence that gamma-ray bursts must come from beyond the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vedrenne_p._16.E2.80.9340_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vedrenne_p._16.E2.80.9340-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Piran92_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Piran92-16">[16]</a></sup> However, some Milky Way models are still consistent with an isotropic distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vedrenne_p._16.E2.80.9340_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vedrenne_p._16.E2.80.9340-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lamb_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lamb-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Counterpart_objects_as_candidate_sources">Counterpart objects as candidate sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Counterpart objects as candidate sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For decades after the discovery of GRBs, astronomers searched for a counterpart at other wavelengths: i.e., any astronomical object in positional coincidence with a recently observed burst. Astronomers considered many distinct classes of objects, including <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarfs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulsar" title="Pulsar">pulsars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>, <a href="/wiki/Globular_cluster" title="Globular cluster">globular clusters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quasar" title="Quasar">quasars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seyfert_galaxies" title="Seyfert galaxies">Seyfert galaxies</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/BL_Lac_object" title="BL Lac object">BL Lac objects</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spatial_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-spatial-18">[18]</a></sup> All such searches were unsuccessful,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-790305b_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-790305b-19">[nb 1]</a></sup> and in a few cases particularly well-localized bursts (those whose positions were determined with what was then a high degree of accuracy) could be clearly shown to have no bright objects of any nature consistent with the position derived from the detecting satellites. This suggested an origin of either very faint stars or extremely distant galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> Even the most accurate positions contained numerous faint stars and galaxies, and it was widely agreed that final resolution of the origins of cosmic gamma-ray bursts would require both new satellites and faster communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFishman_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-CFishman-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BeppoSAX.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="219" data-file-width="250" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/BeppoSAX.jpg/220px-BeppoSAX.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8f/BeppoSAX.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BeppoSAX.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Italian–Dutch satellite <a href="/wiki/BeppoSAX" title="BeppoSAX">BeppoSAX</a>, launched in April 1996, provided the first accurate positions of gamma-ray bursts, allowing follow-up observations and identification of the sources.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Afterglow">Afterglow</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Afterglow">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several models for the origin of gamma-ray bursts postulated that the initial burst of gamma rays should be followed by slowly fading emission at longer wavelengths created by collisions between the burst <a href="/wiki/Ejecta" title="Ejecta">ejecta</a> and interstellar gas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> This fading emission would be called the "afterglow." Early searches for this afterglow were unsuccessful, largely because it is difficult to observe a burst's position at longer wavelengths immediately after the initial burst. The breakthrough came in February 1997 when the satellite <a href="/wiki/BeppoSAX" title="BeppoSAX">BeppoSAX</a> detected a gamma-ray burst (<a href="/wiki/GRB_970228" title="GRB 970228">GRB 970228</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grbnames_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-grbnames-24">[nb 2]</a></sup>) and when the X-ray camera was pointed towards the direction from which the burst had originated, it detected fading X-ray emission. The <a href="/wiki/William_Herschel_Telescope" title="William Herschel Telescope">William Herschel Telescope</a> identified a fading optical counterpart 20 hours after the burst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[23]</a></sup> Once the GRB faded, deep imaging was able to identify a faint, distant host galaxy at the location of the GRB as pinpointed by the optical afterglow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vedrenne_p._90_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vedrenne_p._90-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of the very faint luminosity of this galaxy, its exact distance was not measured for several years. Well before then, another major breakthrough occurred with the next event registered by BeppoSAX, <a href="/wiki/GRB_970508" title="GRB 970508">GRB 970508</a>. This event was localized within four hours of its discovery, allowing research teams to begin making observations much sooner than any previous burst. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_spectrum" title="Absorption spectrum">spectrum</a> of the object revealed a <a href="/wiki/Redshift" title="Redshift">redshift</a> of <i>z</i> = 0.835, placing the burst at a distance of roughly 6 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_year" title="Light year">light years</a> from Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[26]</a></sup> This was the first accurate determination of the distance to a GRB, and together with the discovery of the host galaxy of 970228 proved that GRBs occur in extremely distant galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vedrenne_p._90_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vedrenne_p._90-26">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup> Within a few months, the controversy about the distance scale ended: GRBs were extragalactic events originating within faint galaxies at enormous distances. The following year, <a href="/wiki/GRB_980425" title="GRB 980425">GRB 980425</a> was followed within a day by a coincident bright supernova (<a href="/wiki/SN_1998bw" title="SN 1998bw">SN 1998bw</a>), indicating a clear connection between GRBs and the deaths of very massive stars. This burst provided the first strong clue about the nature of the systems that produce GRBs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98bw_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-98bw-30">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Swift_spacecraft.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="225" data-file-width="250" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Swift_spacecraft.jpg/220px-Swift_spacecraft.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/Swift_spacecraft.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Swift_spacecraft.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Swift_Gamma-Ray_Burst_Mission" title="Swift Gamma-Ray Burst Mission">Swift Spacecraft</a> launched in November 2004</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>BeppoSAX functioned until 2002 and <a href="/wiki/Compton_Gamma_Ray_Observatory" title="Compton Gamma Ray Observatory">CGRO</a> (with BATSE) was deorbited in 2000. However, the revolution in the study of gamma-ray bursts motivated the development of a number of additional instruments designed specifically to explore the nature of GRBs, especially in the earliest moments following the explosion. The first such mission, <a href="/wiki/High_Energy_Transient_Explorer" title="High Energy Transient Explorer">HETE-2</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup> launched in 2000 and functioned until 2006, providing most of the major discoveries during this period. One of the most successful space missions to date, <a href="/wiki/Swift_Gamma-Ray_Burst_Mission" title="Swift Gamma-Ray Burst Mission">Swift</a>, was launched in 2004 and as of 2016 is still operational.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup> Swift is equipped with a very sensitive gamma ray detector as well as on-board X-ray and optical telescopes, which can be rapidly and automatically <a href="/wiki/Slew_(spacecraft)" title="Slew (spacecraft)">slewed</a> to observe afterglow emission following a burst. More recently, the <a href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Space_Telescope" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Space Telescope">Fermi</a> mission was launched carrying the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermi_Gamma-ray_Burst_Monitor" title="Fermi Gamma-ray Burst Monitor">Gamma-Ray Burst Monitor</a>, which detects bursts at a rate of several hundred per year, some of which are bright enough to be observed at extremely high energies with Fermi's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fermi_LAT" title="Fermi LAT">Large Area Telescope</a>. Meanwhile, on the ground, numerous optical telescopes have been built or modified to incorporate robotic control software that responds immediately to signals sent through the <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_Burst_Coordinates_Network" title="Gamma-ray Burst Coordinates Network">Gamma-ray Burst Coordinates Network</a>. This allows the telescopes to rapidly repoint towards a GRB, often within seconds of receiving the signal and while the gamma-ray emission itself is still ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>New developments over the past few years include the recognition of short gamma-ray bursts as a separate class (likely from merging neutron stars and not associated with supernovae), the discovery of extended, erratic flaring activity at X-ray wavelengths lasting for many minutes after most GRBs, and the discovery of the most luminous (<a href="/wiki/GRB_080319B" title="GRB 080319B">GRB 080319B</a>) and the former most distant (<a href="/wiki/GRB_090423" title="GRB 090423">GRB 090423</a>) objects in the universe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloom_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-36">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup> The most distant known GRB, <a href="/wiki/GRB_090429B" title="GRB 090429B">GRB 090429B</a>, is now the most distant known object in the universe.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="908" data-file-width="920" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png/200px-GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png/300px-GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png/400px-GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GRB_BATSE_12lightcurves.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gamma-ray burst light curves</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Light_curve" title="Light curve">light curves</a> of gamma-ray bursts are extremely diverse and complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> No two gamma-ray burst light curves are identical,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[37]</a></sup> with large variation observed in almost every property: the duration of observable emission can vary from milliseconds to tens of minutes, there can be a single peak or several individual subpulses, and individual peaks can be symmetric or with fast brightening and very slow fading. Some bursts are preceded by a "precursor" event, a weak burst that is then followed (after seconds to minutes of no emission at all) by the much more intense "true" bursting episode.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lazzati_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lazzati-40">[38]</a></sup> The light curves of some events have extremely chaotic and complicated profiles with almost no discernible patterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFishman_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-CFishman-22">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although some light curves can be roughly reproduced using certain simplified models,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[39]</a></sup> little progress has been made in understanding the full diversity observed. Many classification schemes have been proposed, but these are often based solely on differences in the appearance of light curves and may not always reflect a true physical difference in the progenitors of the explosions. However, plots of the distribution of the observed duration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-T90_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-T90-42">[nb 3]</a></sup> for a large number of gamma-ray bursts show a clear <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bimodal_distribution" title="Bimodal distribution">bimodality</a>, suggesting the existence of two separate populations: a "short" population with an average duration of about 0.3 seconds and a "long" population with an average duration of about 30 seconds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kouveliotou_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kouveliotou-43">[40]</a></sup> Both distributions are very broad with a significant overlap region in which the identity of a given event is not clear from duration alone. Additional classes beyond this two-tiered system have been proposed on both observational and theoretical grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horvath98_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath98-44">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hakkila_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hakkila-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chattopadhyay_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chattopadhyay-46">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Virgili_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Virgili-47">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short_gamma-ray_bursts">Short gamma-ray bursts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Short gamma-ray bursts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg/220px-Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg/330px-Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg/440px-Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hubble_captures_infrared_glow_of_a_kilonova_blast.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hubble captures infrared glow of a <a href="/wiki/Kilonova" title="Kilonova">kilonova</a> blast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Events with a duration of less than about two seconds are classified as short gamma-ray bursts. These account for about 30% of gamma-ray bursts, but until 2005, no afterglow had been successfully detected from any short event and little was known about their origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-r1-49">[46]</a></sup> Since then, several dozen short gamma-ray burst afterglows have been detected and localized, several of which are associated with regions of little or no star formation, such as large <a href="/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy" title="Elliptical galaxy">elliptical galaxies</a> and the central regions of large <a href="/wiki/Galaxy_cluster" title="Galaxy cluster">galaxy clusters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup> This rules out a link to massive stars, confirming that short events are physically distinct from long events. In addition, there has been no association with supernovae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang-54">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>The true nature of these objects (or even whether the current classification scheme is accurate) remains unknown, although the leading hypothesis is that they originate from the mergers of binary neutron stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> or a neutron star with a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a>. Such mergers might also be expected to produce <a href="/wiki/Kilonova" title="Kilonova">kilonovae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzger2010_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metzger2010-56">[53]</a></sup> and evidence for a kilonova associated with GRB 130603B has been seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tanvir2013_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tanvir2013-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berger2013_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berger2013-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dnews_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dnews-59">[56]</a></sup> The mean duration of these events of 0.2 seconds suggests a source of very small physical diameter in stellar terms; less than 0.2 light-seconds (about 60,000 km or 37,000 miles—four times the Earth's diameter). This further suggests a very compact object as the source.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Need a reliable source explicitly stating 0.2 second events implies a compact source (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The observation of minutes to hours of X-ray flashes after a short gamma-ray burst is consistent with small particles of a primary object like a neutron star initially swallowed by a black hole in less than two seconds, followed by some hours of lesser energy events, as remaining fragments of tidally disrupted neutron star material (no longer <a href="/wiki/Neutronium" title="Neutronium">neutronium</a>) remain in orbit to spiral into the black hole, over a longer period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r1_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-r1-49">[46]</a></sup> A small fraction of short gamma-ray bursts are probably produced by giant flares from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soft_gamma_repeaters" title="Soft gamma repeaters">soft gamma repeaters</a> in nearby galaxies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frederiks_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frederiks-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Long_gamma-ray_bursts">Long gamma-ray bursts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Long gamma-ray bursts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most observed events (70%) have a duration of greater than two seconds and are classified as long gamma-ray bursts. Because these events constitute the majority of the population and because they tend to have the brightest afterglows, they have been studied in much greater detail than their short counterparts. Almost every well-studied long gamma-ray burst has been linked to a galaxy with rapid star formation, and in many cases to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Core-collapse_supernova" title="Core-collapse supernova">core-collapse supernova</a> as well, unambiguously associating long GRBs with the deaths of massive stars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woosley06_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woosley06-62">[59]</a></sup> Long GRB afterglow observations, at high redshift, are also consistent with the GRB having originated in star-forming regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pontzen_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pontzen-63">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ultra-long_gamma-ray_bursts">Ultra-long gamma-ray bursts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ultra-long gamma-ray bursts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>These events are at the tail end of the long GRB duration distribution, lasting more than 10,000 seconds. They have been proposed to form a separate class, possibly the result of the collapse of a blue supergiant star.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> Only a small number have been identified to date, their primary characteristic being their gamma ray emission duration. So far, the known and well established ultra long GRBs are GRB 091024A, GRB 101225A, and GRB 111209A.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[63]</a></sup> A recent study,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[64]</a></sup> on the other hand, shows that the existing evidence for a separate ultra-long GRB population with a new type of progenitor is inconclusive, and further multi-wavelength observations are needed to draw a firmer conclusion.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Energetics_and_beaming">Energetics and beaming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Energetics and beaming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="781" data-file-width="800" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg/250px-GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg/375px-GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg/500px-GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GRB080319B_illustration_NASA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's illustration of a bright gamma-ray burst occurring in a star-forming region. Energy from the explosion is beamed into two narrow, oppositely directed jets.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gamma-ray bursts are very bright as observed from Earth despite their typically immense distances. An average long GRB has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bolometric_magnitude" title="Bolometric magnitude">bolometric</a> flux comparable to a bright star of our galaxy despite a distance of billions of light years (compared to a few tens of light years for most visible stars). Most of this energy is released in gamma rays, although some GRBs have extremely luminous optical counterparts as well. <a href="/wiki/GRB_080319B" title="GRB 080319B">GRB 080319B</a>, for example, was accompanied by an optical counterpart that peaked at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_magnitude" title="Visible magnitude">visible magnitude</a> of 5.8,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Racusin_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Racusin-68">[65]</a></sup> comparable to that of the dimmest naked-eye stars despite the burst's distance of 7.5 billion light years. This combination of brightness and distance implies an extremely energetic source. Assuming the gamma-ray explosion to be spherical, the energy output of GRB 080319B would be within a factor of two of the <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass–energy equivalence">rest-mass energy</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> (the energy which would be released were the Sun to be converted entirely into radiation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloom_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom-36">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>No known process in the universe can produce this much energy in such a short time. Rather, gamma-ray bursts are thought to be highly focused explosions, with most of the explosion energy collimated into a narrow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">jet</a> traveling at speeds exceeding 99.995%<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (May 2016)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[67]</a></sup> The approximate angular width of the jet (that is, the degree of spread of the beam) can be estimated directly by observing the achromatic "jet breaks" in afterglow light curves: a time after which the slowly decaying afterglow begins to fade rapidly as the jet slows and can no longer <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_beaming" title="Relativistic beaming">beam</a> its radiation as effectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sari99_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sari99-71">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup> Observations suggest significant variation in the jet angle from between 2 and 20 degrees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frail_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frail-73">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because their energy is strongly focused, the gamma rays emitted by most bursts are expected to miss the Earth and never be detected. When a gamma-ray burst is pointed towards Earth, the focusing of its energy along a relatively narrow beam causes the burst to appear much brighter than it would have been were its energy emitted spherically. When this effect is taken into account, typical gamma-ray bursts are observed to have a true energy release of about 10<sup>44</sup> J, or about 1/2000 of a <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">Solar mass</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a>) energy equivalent<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frail_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frail-73">[70]</a></sup>—which is still many times the mass-energy equivalent of the Earth (about 5.5 × 10<sup>41</sup> J). This is comparable to the energy released in a bright <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_Ib_supernova" title="Type Ib supernova">type Ib/c</a> <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> and within the range of theoretical models. Very bright supernovae have been observed to accompany several of the nearest GRBs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98bw_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-98bw-30">[28]</a></sup> Additional support for focusing of the output of GRBs has come from observations of strong asymmetries in the spectra of nearby <a href="/wiki/Type_Ib_and_Ic_supernovae" title="Type Ib and Ic supernovae">type Ic supernova</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup> and from radio observations taken long after bursts when their jets are no longer relativistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Short (time duration) GRBs appear to come from a lower-redshift (i.e. less distant) population and are less luminous than long GRBs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHBhosts_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-SHBhosts-76">[73]</a></sup> The degree of beaming in short bursts has not been accurately measured, but as a population they are likely less collimated than long GRBs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> or possibly not collimated at all in some cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Progenitors">Progenitors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Progenitors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst_progenitors" title="Gamma-ray burst progenitors">Gamma-ray burst progenitors</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wolf_rayet2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="641" data-file-width="611" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/220px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/330px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Wolf_rayet2.jpg/440px-Wolf_rayet2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wolf_rayet2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> image of <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet star</a> <a href="/wiki/WR_124" title="WR 124">WR 124</a> and its surrounding nebula. Wolf–Rayet stars are candidates for being progenitors of long-duration GRBs.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of the immense distances of most gamma-ray burst sources from Earth, identification of the progenitors, the systems that produce these explosions, is challenging. The association of some long GRBs with supernovae and the fact that their host galaxies are rapidly star-forming offer very strong evidence that long gamma-ray bursts are associated with massive stars. The most widely accepted mechanism for the origin of long-duration GRBs is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypernova" title="Hypernova">collapsar</a> model,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[76]</a></sup> in which the core of an extremely massive, low-<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>, rapidly rotating star collapses into a <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">black hole</a> in the final stages of its <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolution</a>. Matter near the star's core rains down towards the center and swirls into a high-density <a href="/wiki/Accretion_disk" title="Accretion disk">accretion disk</a>. The infall of this material into a black hole drives a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_jet" title="Relativistic jet">relativistic jets</a> out along the rotational axis, which pummel through the stellar envelope and eventually break through the stellar surface and radiate as gamma rays. Some alternative models replace the black hole with a newly formed <a href="/wiki/Magnetar" title="Magnetar">magnetar</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> although most other aspects of the model (the collapse of the core of a massive star and the formation of relativistic jets) are the same.</p>
<p>The closest analogs within the Milky Way galaxy of the stars producing long gamma-ray bursts are likely the <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet stars</a>, extremely hot and massive stars, which have shed most or all of their hydrogen to <a href="/wiki/Radiation_pressure" title="Radiation pressure">radiation pressure</a>. <a href="/wiki/Eta_Carinae" title="Eta Carinae">Eta Carinae</a> and <a href="/wiki/WR_104" title="WR 104">WR 104</a> have been cited as possible future gamma-ray burst progenitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup> It is unclear if any star in the Milky Way has the appropriate characteristics to produce a gamma-ray burst.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanek_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanek-83">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The massive-star model probably does not explain all types of gamma-ray burst. There is strong evidence that some short-duration gamma-ray bursts occur in systems with no star formation and no massive stars, such as elliptical galaxies and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galaxy_halo" title="Galaxy halo">galaxy halos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHBhosts_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-SHBhosts-76">[73]</a></sup> The favored theory for the origin of most short gamma-ray bursts is the merger of a binary system consisting of two neutron stars. According to this model, the two stars in a binary slowly spiral towards each other because <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_radiation" title="Gravitational radiation">gravitational radiation</a> releases energy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> until <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tidal_forces" title="Tidal forces">tidal forces</a> suddenly rip the neutron stars apart and they collapse into a single black hole. The infall of matter into the new black hole produces an accretion disk and releases a burst of energy, analogous to the collapsar model. Numerous other models have also been proposed to explain short gamma-ray bursts, including the merger of a neutron star and a black hole, the accretion-induced collapse of a neutron star, or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Black_hole_evaporation" title="Black hole evaporation">evaporation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Primordial_black_hole" title="Primordial black hole">primordial black holes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cline_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-cline-89">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>An alternative explanation proposed by <a href="/wiki/Friedwardt_Winterberg" title="Friedwardt Winterberg">Friedwardt Winterberg</a> is that in the course of a gravitational collapse and in reaching the event horizon of a black hole, all matter disintegrates into a burst of gamma radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tidal_disruption_events">Tidal disruption events</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tidal disruption events">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tidal_disruption_event" title="Tidal disruption event">Tidal disruption event</a></div>
<p>This new class of GRB-like events was first discovered through the detection of <a href="/wiki/GRB_110328A" title="GRB 110328A">GRB 110328A</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Swift_Gamma-Ray_Burst_Mission" title="Swift Gamma-Ray Burst Mission">Swift Gamma-Ray Burst Mission</a> on 28 March 2011. This event had a gamma-ray duration of about 2 days, much longer than even ultra-long GRBs, and was detected in X-rays for many months. It occurred at the center of a small elliptical galaxy at redshift z = 0.3534. There is an ongoing debate as to whether the explosion was the result of stellar collapse or a tidal disruption event accompanied by a relativistic jet, although the latter explanation has become widely favoured.</p>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Tidal_disruption_event" title="Tidal disruption event">tidal disruption event</a> of this sort is when a star interacts with a <a href="/wiki/Supermassive_black_hole" title="Supermassive black hole">supermassive black hole</a> shredding the star, and in some cases creating a relativistic jet which produces bright emission of gamma ray radiation. The event <a href="/wiki/GRB_110328A" title="GRB 110328A">GRB 110328A</a> (also denoted Swift J1644+57) was initially argued to be produced by the disruption of main sequence star by a black hole of several million times the mass of the Sun,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levan11_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Levan11-92">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloom11_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloom11-93">[90]</a></sup> although it has subsequently been argued that the disruption of a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> by a black hole of mass about 10 thousand times the Sun may be more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Emission_mechanisms">Emission mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Emission mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst_emission_mechanisms" title="Gamma-ray burst emission mechanisms">Gamma-ray burst emission mechanisms</a></div>
<p>The means by which gamma-ray bursts convert energy into radiation remains poorly understood, and as of 2010 there was still no generally accepted model for how this process occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup> Any successful model of GRB emission must explain the physical process for generating gamma-ray emission that matches the observed diversity of light curves, spectra, and other characteristics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> Particularly challenging is the need to explain the very high efficiencies that are inferred from some explosions: some gamma-ray bursts may convert as much as half (or more) of the explosion energy into gamma-rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup> Early observations of the bright optical counterparts to <a href="/wiki/GRB_990123" title="GRB 990123">GRB 990123</a> and to <a href="/wiki/GRB_080319B" title="GRB 080319B">GRB 080319B</a>, whose optical light curves were extrapolations of the gamma-ray light spectra,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Racusin_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Racusin-68">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup> have suggested that <a href="/wiki/Compton_scattering" title="Compton scattering">inverse Compton</a> may be the dominant process in some events. In this model, pre-existing low-energy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photons" title="Photons">photons</a> are scattered by relativistic electrons within the explosion, augmenting their energy by a large factor and transforming them into gamma-rays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>The nature of the longer-wavelength afterglow emission (ranging from <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_waves" title="Radio waves">radio</a>) that follows gamma-ray bursts is better understood. Any energy released by the explosion not radiated away in the burst itself takes the form of matter or energy moving outward at nearly the speed of light. As this matter collides with the surrounding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstellar_gas" title="Interstellar gas">interstellar gas</a>, it creates a <a href="/wiki/Relativistic_speed" title="Relativistic speed">relativistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Shock_wave" title="Shock wave">shock wave</a> that then propagates forward into interstellar space. A second shock wave, the reverse shock, may propagate back into the ejected matter. Extremely energetic electrons within the shock wave are accelerated by strong local magnetic fields and radiate as <a href="/wiki/Synchrotron" title="Synchrotron">synchrotron</a> emission across most of the <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum" title="Electromagnetic spectrum">electromagnetic spectrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[98]</a></sup> This model has generally been successful in modeling the behavior of many observed afterglows at late times (generally, hours to days after the explosion), although there are difficulties explaining all features of the afterglow very shortly after the gamma-ray burst has occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rate_of_occurrence_and_potential_effects_on_life">Rate of occurrence and potential effects on life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Rate of occurrence and potential effects on life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1275" data-file-width="1280" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg/220px-GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg/330px-GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg/440px-GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GROND_image_of_the_gamma-ray_burst_GRB_151027B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
On 27 October 2015, at 22:40 GMT, the NASA/ASI/UKSA Swift satellite discovered its 1000th gamma-ray burst (GRB).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[100]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gamma ray bursts can have harmful or destructive effects on life. Considering the universe as a whole, the safest environments for life similar to that on Earth are the lowest density regions in the outskirts of large galaxies. Our knowledge of <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a> types and their distribution suggests that life as we know it can only exist in about 10% of all galaxies. Furthermore, galaxies with a redshift, <i>z</i>, higher than 0.5 are unsuitable for life as we know it, because of their higher rate of GRBs and their stellar compactness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>All GRBs observed to date have occurred well outside the Milky Way galaxy and have been harmless to Earth. However, if a GRB were to occur within the Milky Way and its emission were beamed straight towards Earth, the effects could be harmful and potentially devastating for the <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>. Currently, orbiting satellites detect on average approximately one GRB per day. The closest observed GRB as of March 2014 was <a href="/wiki/GRB_980425" title="GRB 980425">GRB 980425</a>, located 40 megaparsecs (130,000,000 ly)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[103]</a></sup> away (<a href="/wiki/Redshift#Measurement.2C_characterization.2C_and_interpretation" title="Redshift">z</a>=0.0085) in a SBc-type dwarf galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[104]</a></sup> GRB 980425 was far less energetic than the average GRB and was associated with the <a href="/wiki/Type_Ib_and_Ic_supernovae" title="Type Ib and Ic supernovae">Type Ib supernova</a> <a href="/wiki/SN_1998bw" title="SN 1998bw">SN 1998bw</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kippen_et_al._.281998.29_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kippen_et_al._.281998.29-108">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>Estimating the exact rate at which GRBs occur is difficult; for a galaxy of approximately the same size as the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, estimates of the expected rate (for long-duration GRBs) can range from one burst every 10,000 years, to one burst every 1,000,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[106]</a></sup> Only a small percentage of these would be beamed towards Earth. Estimates of rate of occurrence of short-duration GRBs are even more uncertain because of the unknown degree of collimation, but are probably comparable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since GRBs are thought to involve beamed emission along two jets in opposing directions, only planets in the path of these jets would be subjected to the high energy gamma radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[108]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although nearby GRBs hitting Earth with a destructive shower of gamma rays are only hypothetical events, high energy processes in the nearby universe are well-known to affect the Earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_Earth">Effects on Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Effects on Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Earth's atmosphere is very effective at absorbing high energy electromagnetic radiation such as x-rays and gamma rays, so these types of radiation would not reach any dangerous levels at the surface during the burst event itself. The immediate effect on life on Earth from a GRB within a few <a href="/wiki/Parsec" title="Parsec">parsecs</a> would only be a short increase in ultraviolet radiation at ground level, lasting from less than a second to tens of seconds. This ultraviolet radiation could potentially reach dangerous levels depending on the exact nature and distance of the burst, but it seems unlikely to be able to cause a global catastrophe for life on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Threat_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Threat-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Effects-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The long term effects from a nearby burst are more dangerous. Gamma rays cause chemical reactions in the atmosphere involving <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecules" title="Molecules">molecules</a>, creating first <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a> then <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> gas. The nitrogen oxides cause dangerous effects on three levels. First, they deplete <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone</a>, with models showing a possible global reduction of 25-35%, with as much as 75% in certain locations, an effect that would last for years. This reduction is enough to cause a dangerously elevated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UV_index" title="UV index">UV index</a> at the surface. Secondly, the nitrogen oxides cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photochemical_smog" title="Photochemical smog">photochemical smog</a>, which darkens the sky and blocks out parts of the <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a> spectrum. This would affect <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, but models show only about a 1% reduction of the total sunlight spectrum, lasting a few years. However, the smog could potentially cause a cooling effect on Earth's climate, producing a "cosmic winter" (similar to an <a href="/wiki/Impact_winter" title="Impact winter">impact winter</a>, but without an impact), but only if it occurs simultaneously with a global climate instability. Thirdly, the elevated nitrogen levels in the atmosphere would wash out and produce <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">nitric acid rain</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">Nitric acid</a> is toxic to a variety of organisms, including amphibian life, but models show that it cannot reach levels that would cause a serious global effect. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitrates" title="Nitrates">nitrates</a> might in fact be of benefit to some plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Threat_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Threat-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-Effects-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>All in all, a GRB within a few parsecs, with its energy directed towards Earth, will mostly damage life by raising the UV levels; during the burst itself and for a few years thereafter. Models shows that the destructive effects of this increase can cause up to 16 times the normal levels of DNA damage. It has proved difficult to assess a reliable evaluation of the consequences of this on the terrestrial ecosystem, because of the uncertainty in biological field and laboratory data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Threat_113-2"><a href="#cite_note-Threat-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_114-2"><a href="#cite_note-Effects-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ordinary <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a> explosions can have the same effects as GRBs on Earth's atmosphere if they are close enough.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="how close is that? (November 2016)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Locally, supernovas are much more frequent events than GRBs, with a possibility of occurring closer to Earth. In the field of <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a>, the rate and distribution of supernovas are used to define what is known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galactic_Habitable_Zone" title="Galactic Habitable Zone">Galactic Habitable Zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effects_114-3"><a href="#cite_note-Effects-114">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hypothetical_effects_on_Earth_in_the_past">Hypothetical effects on Earth in the past</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Hypothetical effects on Earth in the past">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>GRBs close enough to affect life in some way might occur once every five million years or so – around a thousand times since life on Earth began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[112]</a></sup></p>
<p>The major <a href="/wiki/Ordovician%E2%80%93Silurian_extinction_events" title="Ordovician–Silurian extinction events">Ordovician–Silurian extinction events</a> 450 million years ago may have been caused by a GRB. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Ordovician" title="Late Ordovician">late Ordovician</a> species of <a href="/wiki/Trilobite" title="Trilobite">trilobite</a> that spent some of its life in the plankton layer near the ocean surface was much harder hit than deep-water dwellers, which tended to remain within quite restricted areas. This is in contrast to the usual effects of extinction events, when more widely spread species fared better. This unusual pattern could <a href="/wiki/Hypothesis" title="Hypothesis">hypothetically</a> be explained by a GRB, since it would probably devastate creatures living on land and near the ocean surface, but leave deep-sea creatures relatively unharmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Melott2004_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Melott2004-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>A case has been made that the <a href="/wiki/774%E2%80%93775_carbon-14_spike" title="774–775 carbon-14 spike">774–775 carbon-14 spike</a> was the result of a short GRB,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pavlov_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-pavlov-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hamb_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-hamb-117">[114]</a></sup> though a very strong <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flare</a> is another possibility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mek15_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-mek15-118">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="WR_104:_A_nearby_GRB_candidate">WR 104: A nearby GRB candidate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: WR 104: A nearby GRB candidate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/WR_104#Supernova_explosions" title="WR 104">WR 104 § Supernova explosions</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet star</a> in <a href="/wiki/WR_104" title="WR 104">WR 104</a>, about 8,000 light-years (2,500 pc) away, is considered a nearby GRB candidate that could have destructive effects on terrestrial life. It is expected to explode in a core-collapse-supernova at some point within the next 500,000 years and it is possible that this explosion will create a GRB. If that happens, there is a small chance that Earth will be in the path of its gamma ray jet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tuthill_subject_expert_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-tuthill_subject_expert-119">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-time_super_duper_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-time_super_duper-120">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tuthill_subject_expert_faq_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-tuthill_subject_expert_faq-121">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="GRB_candidates_in_the_Milky_Way">GRB candidates in the Milky Way</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: GRB candidates in the Milky Way">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>No gamma-ray burst from within our own galaxy, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy" title="Milky Way Galaxy">Milky Way</a>, has been observed, and the question of whether one has ever occurred remains unresolved. In light of evolving understanding of gamma-ray bursts and their progenitors, the scientific literature records a growing number of local, past, and future GRB candidates. Long duration GRBs are related to superluminous supernovae, or hypernovae, and most <a href="/wiki/Luminous_blue_variable" title="Luminous blue variable">luminous blue variables</a> (LBVs), and rapidly spinning <a href="/wiki/Wolf%E2%80%93Rayet_star" title="Wolf–Rayet star">Wolf–Rayet stars</a> are believed to end their life cycles in core-collapse supernovae with an associated long-duration GRB. Knowledge of GRBs, however, is from metal-poor galaxies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_universe" title="History of the universe">former epochs of the universe's evolution</a>, and it is impossible to directly extrapolate to encompass more evolved galaxies and stellar environments with a higher <a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metallicity</a>, such as the Milky Way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gamma-ray_burst&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Footnotes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-790305b-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-790305b_19-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A notable exception is the <a href="/wiki/GRB_790305b" title="GRB 790305b">5 March event</a> of 1979, an extremely bright burst that was successfully localized to supernova remnant <a href="/wiki/LMC_N49" title="LMC N49">N49</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Large_Magellanic_Cloud" title="Large Magellanic Cloud">Large Magellanic Cloud</a>. This event is now interpreted as a <a href="/wiki/Magnetar" title="Magnetar">magnetar</a> <a href="/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst_progenitors#Magnetar_giant_flares" title="Gamma-ray burst progenitors">giant flare</a>, more related to <a href="/wiki/Soft_gamma_repeater" title="Soft gamma repeater">SGR</a> flares than "true" gamma-ray bursts.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-grbnames-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-grbnames_24-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">GRBs are named after the date on which they are discovered: the first two digits being the year, followed by the two-digit month and two-digit day and a letter with the order they were detected during that day. The letter 'A' is appended to the name for the first burst identified, 'B' for the second, and so on. For bursts before the year 2010 this letter was only appended if more than one burst occurred that day.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-T90-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-T90_42-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The duration of a burst is typically measured by T90, the duration of the period which 90 percent of the burst's <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> is emitted. Recently some otherwise "short" GRBs have been shown to be followed by a second, much longer emission episode that when included in the burst light curve results in T90 durations of up to several minutes: these events are only short in the literal sense when this component is excluded.</span></li>
</ol>

<th class="navbox-group" scope="row">Types</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
